2:23:1316:13:35:465:524:662:11:Times:11:Times:F:January 1994
2:24:1328:35:12:222:405:627:11:Times:11:Times:F:PREDICTABLE RISK AND RETURNS
2:25:1356:12:38:249:377:615:11:Times:11:Times:F:IN EMERGING MARKETS
2:26:1375:38:30:283:343:577:12:Times:12:Times:F:ABSTRACT
2:27:1383:30:19:133:524:547:11:Times:8:Courier:F:The emergence of newequitymarkets in Europe, Latin America,Asia, theMideastand
2:28:1461:19:19:101:527:528:11:Times:11:Times:F:Africa provides a new menu of opportunities for investors. These markets exhibit high expected
2:29:1555:19:19:101:526:509:12:Times:12:Times:F:returns as well as high volatility. Importantly, the low correlations with developed countries'
2:30:1650:19:19:101:524:490:12:Times:12:Times:F:equity markets significantly reduces the unconditional portfolio risk of a world investor.
2:31:1740:19:18:101:527:471:11:Times:11:Times:F:However, standard global asset pricing models, which assume complete integration of capital
2:32:1831:18:19:101:524:453:11:Times:11:Times:F:markets, fail to explain the cross-section of average returns in emerging countries. An analysis
2:33:1927:19:18:101:526:434:12:Times:12:Times:F:of the predictability of the returns reveals that emerging market returns are more likely than
2:34:2021:18:57:101:363:416:11:Times:11:Times:F:developed countries to be influenced by local information.
2:35:2079:57:10:101:193:359:11:Times:11:Times:F:Campbell R. Harvey
2:36:2097:10:14:101:218:349:11:Times:11:Times:F:Fuqua School of Business
2:37:2121:14:11:101:174:335:11:Times:11:Times:F:Duke University
2:38:2136:11:13:101:150:324:11:Times:9:Courier:F:Box90120
2:39:2144:13:12:102:214:311:9:Courier:9:Courier:F:Durham, NC 27708-0 120
2:40:2166:12:0:102:150:299:10:Courier:10:Courier:F:and NBER
3:41:2174:0:32:102:194:698:16:Times:16:Times:C:1. Introduction
3:42:2189:32:19:125:530:666:14:Times:14:Times:C:In recent years, a number of new equity markets have emerged in Europe,
3:43:2260:19:19:101:528:647:13:Times:13:Times:B:Latin America, Asia, the Mideast and Africa. Little is known about these markets
3:44:2340:19:19:101:528:628:13:Times:13:Times:B:other than the fact that the expected returns can be impressive and these markets
3:45:2421:19:19:101:529:609:14:Times:14:Times:C:are highly volatile. Importantly, the correlations of these equity returns with
3:46:2500:19:20:101:529:590:14:Times:14:Times:C:developed countries' equity returns are low. As a result, it may be possible to
3:47:2579:20:22:101:403:570:13:Times:13:Times:B:lower portfolio risk by participating in emerging markets.
3:48:2637:22:19:125:529:548:14:Times:14:Times:C:This paper has a number of goals. First, the average or unconditional risk
3:49:2711:19:19:101:528:529:13:Times:13:Times:B:of these equity returns is studied. While previous authors have documented low
3:50:2789:19:20:101:528:510:14:Times:14:Times:C:correlations of the emerging market returns with developed country returns, I
3:51:2866:20:18:101:531:490:13:Times:13:Times:B:test whether adding emerging market assets to the portfolio problem significantly
3:52:2947:18:21:101:528:472:13:Times:13:Times:B:shifts the investment opportunity set. I find that the addition of emerging market
3:53:3029:21:22:101:374:451:13:Times:13:Times:B:assets significantly enhances portfolio opportunities.
3:54:3083:22:18:125:528:429:14:Times:14:Times:C:Second, I explore reasons why the emerging market equities have high ex-
3:55:3155:18:19:101:527:411:14:Times:14:Times:C:pected returns. In the framework of asset pricing theory, high expected returns
3:56:3234:19:19:101:528:392:13:Times:13:Times:B:should be associated with large exposures to risk factors. However, I find that the
3:57:3317:19:19:101:530:373:13:Times:13:Times:B:exposures to the commonly used risk factors are low. The asset pricing model, as
3:58:3397:19:19:101:530:354:13:Times:13:Times:B:specified, is unable to explain the cross-section of expected returns. One possible
3:59:3480:19:19:101:528:335:13:Times:13:Times:B:reason for this failure is the implicit assumption of complete integration of world
3:60:3563:19:20:101:528:316:13:Times:13:Times:B:capital markets. Some evidence is offered that suggests that this assumption may
3:61:3643:20:22:101:162:296:13:Times:13:Times:B:he violated.
3:62:3655:22:19:125:530:274:14:Times:14:Times:C:Third, the time-variation in the emerging market returns is studied. Emerg-
3:63:3730:19:20:101:527:255:14:Times:14:Times:C:ing markets contrast with developed markets in at least two respects. I offer
3:64:3807:20:19:101:529:235:14:Times:14:Times:C:evidence that the emerging market returns are generally more predictable than
3:65:3884:19:19:101:529:216:14:Times:14:Times:C:the developed market returns. In addition, it is more likely that the emerging
3:66:3962:19:22:101:521:197:13:Times:13:Times:B:market returns are influenced by local rather than global information variables.
3:67:4042:22:19:126:528:175:13:Times:13:Times:A:One interpretation of the influence of local information is that the emerging
3:68:4119:19:20:101:530:156:14:Times:14:Times:C:markets are segmented from world capital markets. A second interpretation is
3:69:4195:20:19:102:530:136:13:Times:13:Times:B:that there is important time-variation in the risk exposures of the emerging mar-
3:70:4276:19:19:101:530:117:13:Times:13:Times:B:kets. For countries with stable, developed industrial structures, many researchers
3:71:4358:19:33:101:528:98:13:Times:13:Times:B:studying time-varying asset returns have assumed that risk loadings are constant.
3:72:4439:33:0:312:316:65:8:Times:8:Times:F:1
4:73:4440:0:20:142:571:707:13:Times:13:Times:A:This is a far less reasonable assumption for developing countries. The country risk
4:74:4523:20:18:142:570:687:13:Times:13:Times:A:exposure reflects the weighted average of the risk exposures of the companies that
4:75:4605:18:20:142:569:669:14:Times:14:Times:C:are included in the country index. As the industrial structure develops, both the
4:76:4686:20:19:142:567:649:14:Times:14:Times:C:weights and the risk exposures of the individual companies could change. This
4:77:4763:19:19:142:569:630:14:Times:14:Times:C:may induce time-variation in risk exposures. In addition, the risk exposures are
4:78:4843:19:23:141:489:611:13:Times:13:Times:A:likely influenced by local rather than global information variables.
4:79:4911:23:19:165:570:588:14:Times:14:Times:C:I study a conditional asset pricing model where the expected returns are
4:80:4983:19:19:141:567:569:13:Times:13:Times:A:functions of global and local information variables, the world risk premiums are
4:81:5063:19:18:140:569:550:14:Times:14:Times:C:dependent only on global information and the conditional risk is a function of
4:82:5141:18:20:140:568:532:14:Times:14:Times:C:both global and local information. In contrast to the unconditional models, this
4:83:5221:20:17:140:567:512:13:Times:13:Times:A:specification allows for time-variation in the risk exposures and the expected re-
4:84:5303:17:25:140:170:495:13:Times:13:Times:A:turns.
4:85:5309:25:19:164:565:470:14:Times:13:Times:A:Tests of the conditional asset pricing model suggest that the riskexposures
4:86:5384:19:18:139:565:451:13:Times:13:Times:A:significantly change through time for a number of the countries. An examination
4:87:5463:18:20:139:565:433:14:Times:13:Times:A:of the time-varying risk functions suggests that theexposures in some countries
4:88:5542:20:18:139:568:413:13:Times:13:Times:A:move with the time-varying conditional correlations of the countries' returns and
4:89:5623:18:19:139:567:395:14:Times:14:Times:C:a benchmark world return. However, the asset pricing model's restrictions are
4:90:5700:19:17:138:566:376:13:Times:13:Times:A:rejected which means that this formulation is unable to explain the predictability
4:91:5782:17:24:138:431:359:13:Times:13:Times:A:of the returns nor the cross-section of expected returns.
4:92:5839:24:18:162:564:335:13:Times:13:Times:A:The paper is organized as follows. The second section providesa description
4:93:5914:18:21:138:563:317:13:Times:13:Times:A:of the data and some summary statistics for the 20emerging market index returns.
4:94:5994:21:19:138:563:296:13:Times:13:Times:A:In the third section, an analysis of the cross-country correlations of theemerging
4:95:6076:19:20:138:563:277:13:Times:13:Times:A:and developed returns is presented. The section includes tests of whetheradding
4:96:6155:20:19:137:563:257:13:Times:13:Times:A:emerging markets to the portfolio problem significantly changes portfoliooppor-
4:97:6234:19:19:137:565:238:13:Times:13:Times:A:tunities. The average risk exposures to prespecified world economic risk factors
4:98:6314:19:19:137:565:219:14:Times:14:Times:C:are also presented and interpreted. The rejection of the asset pricing theory is
4:99:6394:19:18:136:563:200:13:Times:13:Times:A:characterized in mean-variance space. The fourth section details thepredictability
4:100:6476:18:20:136:562:182:14:Times:13:Times:A:of the emerging market returns. Conditional assetpricing models are estimated
4:101:6553:20:19:136:562:162:13:Times:13:Times:A:with the goal of trying to explain the predictability and to understand thesources
4:102:6635:19:68:136:536:143:13:Times:13:Times:A:of model rejection. Some concluding remarks are offered in the final section.
4:103:6712:68:0:345:350:75:10:Times:10:Times:F:2
5:104:6713:0:28:101:491:697:15:Times:15:Times:C:2. Characterizing the returns and volatility of emerging markets
5:105:6777:28:31:101:187:669:13:Times:13:Times:B:2.1 Data sources
5:106:6793:31:21:101:526:638:13:Times:13:Times:B:Data on more than 800 equities in six Latin American markets (Argentina, Brazil,
5:107:6873:21:19:101:526:617:13:Times:13:Times:B:Chile, Colombia, Mexico, Venezuela), eight Asian markets (India, Indonesia, I<o-
5:108:6953:19:17:100:528:598:13:Times:13:Times:B:rea, Malaysia, Pakistan, Philippines, Taiwan, Thailand), three European markets
5:109:7032:17:19:101:526:581:13:Times:13:Times:B:(Greece, Portugal, Turkey), one Mideast market (Jordan) and two African markets
5:110:7111:19:19:101:528:562:13:Times:13:Times:B:(Nigeria, Zimbabwe) form the Emerging Market Data Base (EMDB) of the Inter-
5:111:7186:19:19:100:525:543:14:Times:14:Times:C:national Finance Corporation (IFC) which is part of the World Bank. Monthly
5:112:7261:19:19:101:528:524:13:Times:13:Times:B:value-weighted index returns are calculated, with dividend reinvestment, for these
5:113:7343:19:19:101:528:505:13:Times:13:Times:B:20 countries. These markets are labeled "emerging" as a result their low or middle
5:114:7425:19:19:101:526:486:13:Times:13:Times:B:income status by the World Bank. In 1991, a per capita GNP of US$635 or less
5:115:7501:19:19:101:526:467:13:Times:13:Times:B:implied low income, and per capita GNP between US$636 and US$7,910 defined
5:116:7575:19:23:101:216:448:13:Times:13:Times:B:middle income status.
5:117:7596:23:19:125:527:425:14:Times:14:Times:C:Table 1 provides some basic statistics regarding the composition of the in-
5:118:7671:19:19:100:525:406:13:Times:13:Times:B:dices. For each market in June 1992, the market capitalization in U.S. dollars is
5:119:7752:19:19:100:527:387:14:Times:14:Times:C:provided.' First, the emerging markets are small relative to the U.S., Japan and
5:120:7832:19:18:101:525:368:13:Times:13:Times:B:U.K. equity markets. However, some emerging markets are larger than one might
5:121:7909:18:20:101:529:350:13:Times:13:Times:B:think. For example, capitalizations of Mexico and Taiwan are similar to those of
5:122:7989:20:19:101:525:330:14:Times:14:Times:C:the markets in Italy and the Netherlands. There are ten emerging markets that
5:123:8066:19:20:101:525:311:13:Times:13:Times:B:are larger than the smallest European market (Finland US$13.6 billion). The to-
5:124:8145:20:20:101:528:291:13:Times:13:Times:B:tal capitalization of the emerging markets is US$747.1 billion. This represents 8%
5:125:8227:20:22:101:488:271:13:Times:13:Times:B:of the Morgan Stanley Capital International (MSCI) world capitalization.
5:126:8299:22:19:125:528:249:13:Times:13:Times:A:Similar to the MSCI method for calculating country equity indices, the IFC
5:127:8373:19:19:101:528:230:13:Times:13:Times:B:uses a subset of the stocks trading in the emerging market. Stocks are selected for
5:128:8456:19:19:101:530:211:13:Times:13:Times:B:inclusion in the index based on size, liquidity and industry. The IFC targets 60% of
5:129:8540:19:27:101:526:192:13:Times:13:Times:B:the total market capitalization of the country and 60% of the total trading volume.
5:130:8623:27:13:126:528:165:14:Times:14:Times:C:For most markets, the exchange conversion is based on a rate quoted on
5:131:8693:13:15:101:526:152:14:Times:14:Times:C:the last day of the month in the Wall Street Journal or the Financial Times.
5:132:8769:15:14:102:527:137:13:Times:13:Times:B:When a number of exchange rates exist, the IFC uses the nearest equivalent "free
5:133:8849:14:14:102:528:123:14:Times:14:Times:C:market" rate or a rate that would apply to the repatriation of capital or income.
5:134:8930:14:15:102:526:109:13:Times:13:Times:B:In some cases, even the newspaper rates are not used and the IFC relies on their
5:135:9010:15:28:102:402:94:13:Times:13:Times:B:correspondents in the particular market. See IFC (1993).
5:136:9066:28:0:311:317:66:12:Times:12:Times:F:3
6:137:9067:0:20:139:564:709:13:Times:13:Times:A:The indices do not include stocks whose issuing company is headquartered in an
6:138:9145:20:19:138:567:689:13:Times:13:Times:A:emerging market but listed only on foreign stock exchanges. In addition, if several
6:139:9228:19:19:138:565:670:14:Times:14:Times:C:stocks meet the size and liquidity hurdle, the IFC selects stocks that represent
6:140:9308:19:18:138:567:651:14:Times:14:Times:C:industries which are not well represented in the index. A detailed description of
6:141:9389:18:23:138:537:633:13:Times:13:Times:A:the selection criteria and the index construction is contained in IFC (1993).
6:142:9466:23:19:162:566:610:13:Times:13:Times:A:Table 1 contains the number of stocks used for each of the IFC country indices
6:143:9544:19:20:138:567:591:13:Times:13:Times:A:which ranges from 77 for Korea to 17 in Zimbabwe. These numbers seem small
6:144:9618:20:19:138:565:571:14:Times:14:Times:C:compared to the U.K., Japan and the U.S. However, these portfolio sizes are
6:145:9693:19:19:138:564:552:13:Times:13:Times:A:comparable to the MSCI portfolios for developing countries. For example, Harvey
6:146:9772:19:18:138:564:533:14:Times:14:Times:C:(1991) reports that 15 of the 20 MSCI developed markets have fewer than 77
6:147:9846:18:24:137:324:515:13:Times:13:Times:A:companies included in their indices.
6:148:9882:24:19:162:563:491:13:Times:13:Times:A:Naturally, one might be concerned with the possibility of some biases being
6:149:9957:19:19:137:563:472:13:Times:13:Times:A:introduced as a result of infrequent trading of the some of the index stocks. How-
6:150:10039:19:19:137:563:453:13:Times:13:Times:A:ever, the trading activity of many of the emerging markets is impressive compared
6:151:10120:19:19:137:565:434:13:Times:13:Times:A:to the developed markets. For example, Harvey (1993a) reports that 5 emerging
6:152:10197:19:19:136:563:415:14:Times:14:Times:C:markets have higher turnover than the average turnover in the U.S., Japan and
6:153:10274:19:19:137:562:396:13:Times:13:Times:A:the U.K. and 10 emerging markets have higher turnover than the U.K. However,
6:154:10350:19:17:136:563:377:13:Times:13:Times:A:to mitigate the possible influences of infrequent trading, I concentrate the analysis
6:155:10435:17:55:136:330:360:13:Times:13:Times:A:on monthly rather than weekly data.
6:156:10470:55:31:136:301:305:13:Times:13:Times:A:2.2 Analysis of monthly returns
6:157:10501:31:19:135:563:274:13:Times:13:Times:A:Some summary statistics of the 20 emerging market returns are presented in Table
6:158:10581:19:19:136:560:255:13:Times:13:Times:A:1. In the first panel, all returns are calculated in U.S. dollar terms (translatedusing
6:159:10668:19:20:135:562:236:14:Times:14:Times:C:the effective rate on the last trading day of the month). Annualized arithmetic
6:160:10747:20:19:134:562:216:14:Times:14:Times:C:mean returns range from 71.8% for Argentina to -11.4% for Indonesia (whose
6:161:10821:19:19:134:559:197:13:Times:13:Times:A:sample only begins in February 1990). High average returns are often associated
6:162:10900:19:17:134:559:178:14:Times:14:Times:C:with high volatility. For example, both Argentina and Thrkey have annualized
6:163:10976:17:19:133:559:161:14:Times:14:Times:C:standard deviations over 75%. Taiwan, whoseaverage return is 40.9%, has a
6:164:11049:19:24:133:285:142:13:Times:13:Times:A:standard deviation of 53.9%.
6:165:11077:24:40:157:558:118:13:Times:12:Times:A:Given the high volatility, there will be a sizable divergence between thegeo-
6:166:11154:40:0:342:348:78:12:Times:12:Times:F:4
7:167:11155:0:18:99:525:696:13:Times:13:Times:B:metric and arithmetic average returns. The geometric mean represents the average
7:168:11235:18:18:99:526:678:14:Times:14:Times:C:return to a buy and hold strategy in the particular market with dividend rein-
7:169:11313:18:19:99:526:660:14:Times:14:Times:C:vestment. The geometric mean return for the Latin American Index is 27.6%
7:170:11386:19:20:99:526:641:14:Times:14:Times:C:compared to the arithmetic mean of 35.7%. A less dramatic difference is found
7:171:11463:20:22:100:409:621:13:Times:13:Times:B:for the Asia index, 19.3% arithmetic and 15.7% geometric.
7:172:11520:22:18:124:528:599:13:Times:13:Times:A:In the overall sample, the arithmetic average return on the emerging markets
7:173:11596:18:20:100:526:581:13:Times:13:Times:B:composite index is 20.4% with a standard deviation of 24.9%. The average returns
7:174:11676:20:18:100:526:561:13:Times:13:Times:B:are roughly 50% higher than the MSCI world composite index (13.9% arithmetic,
7:175:11753:18:21:100:526:543:14:Times:14:Times:C:same sample) and the standard deviation is about 80% higher than the MSCI
7:176:11826:21:20:100:210:522:13:Times:13:Times:B:world index (14.4%).
7:177:11846:20:20:125:527:502:14:Times:14:Times:C:Although this study concentrates on U.S. dollar returns, it is informative
7:178:11920:20:20:101:527:482:14:Times:14:Times:C:to consider the magnitude of the local currency returns which are reported in
7:179:11997:20:19:101:527:462:14:Times:14:Times:C:the second panel of table 1. In many countries, especially in Latin America,
7:180:12073:19:19:101:527:443:13:Times:13:Times:B:these returns are dramatically different as a result of high inflation. For example,
7:181:12157:19:19:102:528:424:13:Times:13:Times:B:the average annual return in Argentina is 228.8% in local currency terms with a
7:182:12236:19:21:102:527:405:13:Times:13:Times:B:volatility of 155.2%. The average return in local currency for theBrazilian index
7:183:12317:21:20:102:154:384:12:Times:12:Times:F:is 155.5%.
7:184:12327:20:19:126:529:364:13:Times:13:Times:A:Table 1 also reports the serial correlation of the monthly returns. In contrast
7:185:12406:19:19:102:531:345:13:Times:13:Times:B:to the developed markets, the first-order serial correlation coefficients are higher
7:186:12490:19:19:102:530:326:13:Times:13:Times:B:for the emerging markets. 12 of the 20 emerging markets have serial correlation
7:187:12569:19:20:103:527:307:13:Times:12:Times:B:coefficients greater than 10% and 8 of the markets have coefficients above20%.
7:188:12647:20:20:103:529:287:13:Times:13:Times:B:The first-order autocorrelation in Colombia is an astonishing 49%. The approx-
7:189:12725:20:19:103:528:267:14:Times:13:Times:B:imate standard error for those countries whose data begins in1976:02 is 7.1%.
7:190:12802:19:19:103:528:248:14:Times:14:Times:C:These statistics are in sharp contrast to the three developed markets reportedat
7:191:12882:19:23:103:505:229:13:Times:11:Times:B:the end of table 1 whose first-order serial correlation averages less than1%.2
7:192:12960:23:18:128:529:206:13:Times:13:Times:A:To further investigate the properties of the data, the coefficientsof skewness
7:193:13038:18:19:104:529:188:13:Times:14:Times:B:and excess kurtosis are reported. If the data are normally distributed,then these
7:194:13119:19:55:104:530:169:13:Times:13:Times:B:coefficients should be equal to zero. To test for normality, the following systemof
7:195:13202:55:18:117:531:114:10:Times:14:Times:C:2Harvey(1993a) finds that some of the cross-section of serialcorrelation can
7:196:13278:18:28:105:477:96:13:Times:13:Times:B:be explained by measures of the asset concentration within eachindex.
7:197:13347:28:0:314:320:68:12:Times:12:Times:F:5
8:198:13348:0:24:142:344:709:13:Times:13:Times:A:equations is estimated for each asset i:
8:199:13388:24:23:288:343:685:14:Times:14:Times:C:eit =rit —
8:200:13398:23:23:288:383:662:15:Helvetica:15:Times:C:e2=(r1g p)2 —
8:201:13411:23:0:288:408:639:15:Helvetica:16:Helvetica:C:e3[(rt —p)3J/V2
8:202:13426:0:31:552:566:647:12:Times:12:Times:F:(1)
8:203:13429:31:21:288:419:616:14:Times:10:Helvetica:C:e4 =[(Tjt—— 3
8:204:13442:21:19:141:566:595:11:Helvetica:14:Times:C:wherep is the mean, V is the variance, e ={eite2 e3e4t}represents the
8:205:13511:19:20:141:568:576:14:Times:14:Times:C:disturbances and E[e] =0. Thereare two parameters and four orthogonality
8:206:13583:20:20:141:565:556:13:Times:14:Times:A:conditions leaving a x2testwith two degrees of freedom. The test statistic re-
8:207:13661:20:19:141:566:536:13:Times:13:Times:A:suits from setting the coefficient of skewness and excess kurtosis equal to zero in
8:208:13744:19:17:141:566:517:14:Times:14:Times:C:the third and fourth equations. This forms a joint test of whether these higher
8:209:13823:17:25:140:288:500:13:Times:13:Times:A:moments are equal to zero.3
8:210:13850:25:18:164:565:475:13:Times:13:Times:A:The results suggest that null hypothesis normality can be rejected at the 5%
8:211:13926:18:20:140:567:457:13:Times:13:Times:A:level in 14 of the 20 emerging markets. However, normality cannot be rejected in
8:212:14006:20:19:140:566:437:14:Times:14:Times:C:any of the three developed markets reported. Multivariate tests [not reported in
8:213:14086:19:18:140:567:418:14:Times:14:Times:C:the table] suggest that the emerging markets are not normally distributed. For
8:214:14164:18:19:139:564:400:14:Times:14:Times:C:the eight emerging countries with data from 1976:02, the test statistic with 16
8:215:14243:19:19:139:565:381:14:Times:14:Times:C:degrees of freedom is 68.52 (p-value<0.1%). For the three developed countries
8:216:14320:19:19:139:565:362:14:Times:14:Times:C:over the same sample period, the statistic with 6 degrees of freedom is 9.36 (p-
8:217:14400:19:18:139:564:343:13:Times:13:Times:A:value=15.4%). When the data are sampled from 1986:03, there are 18 emerging
8:218:14475:18:19:138:563:325:14:Times:13:Times:A:market indices and the test statistic is 75.79 (p-value<0.1%). Over thesame
8:219:14550:19:23:138:547:306:13:Times:13:Times:A:period, the test statistic for the developing countries is 12.99 (p-vaiue=4.3%).
8:220:14630:23:19:162:562:283:13:Times:13:Times:A:The summary statistics provide a number of contrasts betweenemerging mar-
8:221:14703:19:20:138:564:264:13:Times:14:Times:A:kets and developed markets. Emerging markets have higheraverage returns and
8:222:14778:20:19:138:564:244:14:Times:14:Times:C:volatility than developed markets. Many of the markets have serial correlation
8:223:14856:19:19:138:564:225:13:Times:13:Times:A:which is much higher than one would expect on the basis of developed markets.
8:224:14933:19:18:137:563:206:14:Times:14:Times:C:Finally, the returns in the emerging markets depart from the normal distribu-
8:225:15010:18:19:137:562:188:14:Times:14:Times:C:tion. These findings will be important in later sections wheninterpreting both
8:226:15088:19:22:137:519:169:13:Times:13:Times:A:the cross-section of average returns and the predictability of the returns.
8:227:15163:22:14:161:561:147:13:Times:13:Times:A:A related test is presented in Richardson and Smith (1993). Theirsystem is
8:228:15237:14:14:136:562:133:13:Times:13:Times:A:contains parameters for the coefficients of skewness and excess kurtosis and the
8:229:15317:14:14:136:561:119:13:Times:13:Times:A:test statistic is analytically solved imposing the null distribution whencalculating
8:230:15401:14:29:136:455:105:13:Times:13:Times:A:the asymptotic variance-covariance matrix of the estimators.
8:231:15461:29:0:345:351:76:12:Times:12:Times:F:6
9:232:15462:0:31:98:380:696:13:Times:13:Times:B:2.3 Survivorship bias in the emerging markets sample
9:233:15514:31:18:98:526:665:14:Times:14:Times:C:There are two potential sources of survivorship bias in the sample of emerging
9:234:15592:18:19:98:524:647:13:Times:13:Times:B:markets. The first concerns the general composition of the countries in the sample.
9:235:15675:19:23:98:468:628:13:Times:13:Times:B:The second source has to do with the way the indices are constructed.
9:236:15744:23:18:123:525:605:13:Times:13:Times:A:In general, there are many possible countries that might have been included
9:237:15819:18:20:98:527:587:13:Times:13:Times:B:in the sample. Indeed, the World Bank considers any stock market in a develop-
9:238:15897:20:19:99:525:567:14:Times:14:Times:C:ing country as an "emerging market." However, the small number of countries
9:239:15972:19:19:98:526:548:13:Times:13:Times:B:that are included the sample are the winners. While the survivorship problem has
9:240:16052:19:19:98:524:529:13:Times:13:Times:B:been extensively studied in biometrics [see, for example, Miller (1976)], the work
9:241:16134:19:20:99:525:510:14:Times:14:Times:C:in finance on this difficult problem is very recent. For example, Brown, Goetz-
9:242:16213:20:21:99:526:490:13:Times:13:Times:B:mann, Ibbotson and Ross (1992) study the impact of survivorship on mutual fund
9:243:16291:21:19:99:167:469:13:Times:13:Times:B:performance.
9:244:16303:19:19:123:526:450:14:Times:14:Times:C:The second source of bias has to do with the way the country indices are
9:245:16375:19:20:99:527:431:13:Times:13:Times:B:constructed. While the IFC does not explicitly select stocks on the basis of histor-
9:246:16459:20:19:99:525:411:13:Times:13:Times:B:ical financial performance or expected future performance, their size and liquidity
9:247:16542:19:19:99:524:392:13:Times:13:Times:B:criteria implicitly reveal information about the past history of the company. This
9:248:16624:19:19:99:527:373:14:Times:14:Times:C:type of survivorship bias in the index stocks, however, will also hold for more
9:249:16703:19:19:99:527:354:14:Times:14:Times:C:conventional indices, such as the MSCI or FT-Actuaries. Kothari, Shanken and
9:250:16779:19:23:100:497:335:13:Times:13:Times:B:Sloan (1992) examine the impact of survivorship bias in asset pricing tests.
9:251:16855:23:18:124:526:312:14:Times:14:Times:C:A more blatant problem is the backtracking of some of the indices. The
9:252:16925:18:21:100:525:294:13:Times:13:Times:B:EMDB was established in early 1981 and the initial indices were based on stocks
9:253:17004:21:19:100:526:273:14:Times:14:Times:C:selected in 1981. For a number of countries, these indices were backtracked to
9:254:17082:19:19:100:525:254:14:Times:14:Times:C:December 1975. The first sixty months of data are potentially plagued with a
9:255:17158:19:19:100:526:235:13:Times:13:Times:B:lookback bias. That is, to be selected in 1981, the companies had to be successful
9:256:17240:19:19:100:527:216:14:Times:14:Times:C:(or at least solvent). As a result, one might expect the first five years of data to
9:257:17324:19:19:100:527:197:13:Times:13:Times:B:reveal high average returns. Indeed, some firms that may have existed in December
9:258:17405:19:19:101:528:178:14:Times:14:Times:C:1975 and that dropped out of the market by January 1981, are not included in
9:259:17481:19:22:100:177:159:13:Times:13:Times:B:the IFC index.
9:260:17495:22:19:125:526:137:13:Times:13:Times:A:The backtracking problem is isolated to the pre-1981 data. In addition, it is
9:261:17572:19:19:101:526:118:14:Times:14:Times:C:not obvious that the problem is that severe. I compared the average returns for
9:262:17651:19:33:101:529:99:13:Times:13:Times:B:1976—1980 to the average returns over 1981-1992. For the eight largest markets
9:263:17729:33:0:311:317:66:12:Times:12:Times:F:7
10:264:17730:0:19:141:568:711:13:Times:13:Times:A:that have data back to December 1975, five have mean returns in the first period
10:265:17810:19:19:141:566:692:14:Times:14:Times:C:that are greater than the second period (when measured in U.s. dollars). This is
10:266:17890:19:19:140:569:673:13:Times:13:Times:A:consistent with survivorship bias. However, if the average returns are computed in
10:267:17972:19:19:141:568:654:13:Times:13:Times:A:local currency terms, only two countries have mean returns in the first period that
10:268:18055:19:20:141:567:635:13:Times:13:Times:A:are greater than the second period. While the importance of survivorship bias is
10:269:18135:20:20:140:566:615:14:Times:14:Times:C:not clear, careful attention is paid later in the paper to separately analyzing the
10:270:18218:20:52:140:436:595:13:Times:13:Times:A:full sample (1976—1992) and a 'no backtracking' sample.
10:271:18273:52:28:141:314:543:15:Times:15:Times:C:3. Average returns and risks
10:272:18301:28:32:141:296:515:13:Times:13:Times:A:3.1 Frontier intersection tests
10:273:18332:32:21:140:566:483:13:Times:13:Times:A:A number of researchers have suggested that the low correlations between emerg-
10:274:18411:21:17:140:567:462:14:Times:14:Times:C:ing markets and developed markets imply portfolio investment opportunities.4
10:275:18487:17:20:140:569:445:13:Times:13:Times:A:However, one obvious question arises: does the addition emerging markets to the
10:276:18566:20:21:140:499:425:13:Times:13:Times:A:portfolio problem significantly shift the investment opportunity set?
10:277:18635:21:18:164:567:404:14:Times:14:Times:C:First, consider the cross-country correlations of the emerging market stock
10:278:18710:18:19:140:566:386:13:Times:13:Times:A:returns in table 2. The sample period is 1986:03—1992:06 (75 observations) for 18
10:279:18791:19:19:140:566:367:14:Times:14:Times:C:markets and shorter samples are reported for Indonesia and Turkey. In contrast
10:280:18869:19:19:140:566:348:13:Times:13:Times:A:to the cross-country correlations of the developed market returns, most of the cor-
10:281:18952:19:19:140:568:329:13:Times:13:Times:A:relations are low and many are negative. Harvey (1991) reports that the average
10:282:19031:19:19:140:566:310:12:Times:12:Times:F:cross-country correlation in 17 developed markets is 41% over the 1970:02—1989:05
10:283:19112:19:21:140:567:291:13:Times:13:Times:A:sample. The average cross-country correlation of the emerging country returns is
10:284:19192:21:17:140:565:270:14:Times:14:Times:C:only 12%. Argentina, Venezuela, Korea, India, Pakistan, Jordan, Nigeria and
10:285:19267:17:20:140:566:253:13:Times:13:Times:A:Zimbabwe have about zero average correlation with the other emerging countries.
10:286:19346:20:19:140:565:233:13:Times:13:Times:A:Surprisingly, Brazil has a negative correlation with Argentina, Venezuela and Mex-
10:287:19428:19:22:139:534:214:13:Times:13:Times:A:ico. Perhaps not surprisingly, India and Pakistan are negatively correlated.
10:288:19504:22:19:164:565:192:13:Times:13:Times:A:Table 2 also reports the average correlation of each of the emerging country
10:289:19580:19:30:139:567:173:13:Times:13:Times:A:returns with the 18 MSCI developed market returns. Similar to the results among
10:290:19659:30:21:152:566:143:28:Times:13:Times:A:'Forexample, see Errunza (1983), Errunza and Padmanabhan (1988), Stone
10:291:19729:21:14:140:565:122:13:Times:13:Times:A:(1990), Divecha, Drach and Stefek (1992), Speidell and Sappenfield (1992) and
10:292:19806:14:28:139:217:108:13:Times:13:Times:A:Wilcox (1992).
10:293:19820:28:0:349:355:80:12:Times:12:Times:F:8
11:294:19821:0:19:102:526:696:13:Times:13:Times:B:the emerging markets, there are many low average correlations between developed
11:295:19900:19:19:102:528:677:13:Times:13:Times:B:and emerging markets. Argentina, Colombia, Venezuela, India, Pakistan, Nigeria
11:296:19978:19:19:102:529:658:13:Times:13:Times:B:and Zimbabwe have effectively zero average correlation with developed markets.
11:297:20056:19:18:102:528:639:13:Times:13:Times:B:The overall average correlation between emerging and developed markets is only
11:298:20134:18:19:102:527:621:13:Times:13:Times:B:14%. The final line reports the correlation of the emerging country returns with
11:299:20214:19:19:102:529:602:14:Times:14:Times:C:the MSCI world market return. Similar to the results which equally-weight the
11:300:20291:19:19:101:530:583:13:Times:13:Times:B:correlations in the 18 developed markets, the average correlation of the emerging
11:301:20372:19:22:102:366:564:13:Times:13:Times:B:markets and the world market return is only 15%.
11:302:20420:22:19:126:529:542:13:Times:13:Times:A:Figure 1 presents unconditional minimum standard deviation frontiers based
11:303:20494:19:19:102:529:523:14:Times:14:Times:C:on data from 1986:03—1992:06. In the first panel, the dotted curve is based on
11:304:20572:19:19:103:527:504:13:Times:13:Times:B:18 MSCI country indices. The solid curve shows the effect of adding 18 emerging
11:305:20651:19:20:102:528:485:14:Times:14:Times:C:country indices to the problem. Indonesia and Turkey are not included in the
11:306:20727:20:19:102:528:465:13:Times:13:Times:B:sample because of their short histories. At the global minimum variance portfolio,
11:307:20809:19:19:102:531:446:13:Times:13:Times:B:the standard deviation is reduced by 6% (from 13% to 7%) by adding the emerging
11:308:20888:19:21:102:177:427:13:Times:13:Times:B:market assets.
11:309:20902:21:20:127:529:406:13:Times:13:Times:A:The second panel of figure 1 repeats the minimum standard deviation anal-
11:310:20975:20:18:103:530:386:14:Times:14:Times:C:ysis with the constraint of no short selling imposed. This may be a particularly
11:311:21055:18:19:103:528:368:13:Times:13:Times:B:appropriate constraint for the emerging market sample where it could be opera-
11:312:21133:19:19:103:529:349:13:Times:13:Times:B:tionally difficult to short a basket of securities. Interestingly, the analysis does not
11:313:21221:19:20:103:530:330:13:Times:13:Times:B:substantially change. The minimum variance portfolio with 18 developed returns
11:314:21299:20:18:104:531:310:14:Times:14:Times:C:has a standard deviation of 14.5%. When the 18 emerging market returns are
11:315:21373:18:24:104:489:292:13:Times:13:Times:B:added, the minimum variance portfolio has a standard deviation of 7.5%.
11:316:21444:24:19:128:531:268:13:Times:13:Times:A:The graphical analysis does not answer the question of whether the frontier
11:317:21519:19:19:104:531:249:14:Times:14:Times:C:significantly shifts when the emerging market assets are added to the problem.
11:318:21597:19:21:104:529:230:13:Times:13:Times:B:Following Shanken (1986), Huberman and Kandel (1987) and Jobson andKorkie
11:319:21670:21:20:105:529:209:14:Times:13:Times:B:(1989) let r{ri,r2} where r1isthe matrix of returns in 18 developed markets
11:320:21745:20:17:105:529:189:14:Times:14:Times:C:and r2representsthe returns in the 18 emerging markets. The test whether one
11:321:21821:17:21:105:530:172:13:Times:13:Times:B:set of assets (developed returns) spans the frontier of both developed and emerging
11:322:21904:21:30:105:397:151:13:Times:13:Times:B:markets by estimating the following moment condition:
11:323:21957:30:28:246:529:121:14:Times:12:Helvetica:C:m= ?2t — a —— trot,(2)
11:324:21979:28:27:105:530:93:13:Times:12:Helvetica:B:where r0isthe return on the minimum variance portfolio constructed from r(all
11:325:22056:27:0:315:321:66:12:Times:12:Times:F:9
12:326:22057:0:18:139:567:710:13:Times:13:Times:A:36 assets), a and 6 are 1 x 18 parameter vectors, /3 is a 18 x 18 parameter matrix, i
12:327:22142:18:21:139:565:692:8:Courier:13:Times:A:definesthe disturbances and E[iiIl, r1,rcj] = 0.Let the set of minimum-variance
12:328:22221:21:18:139:564:671:14:Times:13:Times:A:portfolios generated by r1beefficient with respect to the assets r.FromRoll
12:329:22296:18:20:139:565:653:13:Times:13:Times:A:(1977), we know that a regression of r2onr1andthe global minimum variance
12:330:22369:20:17:139:565:633:13:Times:14:Times:A:portfolio return should yield zero intercepts if r2intersectsthe efficient set. The
12:331:22452:17:23:138:361:616:13:Times:13:Times:A:slope coefficients should also sum to unity.
12:332:22496:23:30:162:552:593:13:Times:13:Times:A:The test statistic due to Shanken (1986) and Jobson and Korkie (1989) is
12:333:22568:30:15:287:559:563:8:Times:12:Times:F:—(T—N—1)(a—a21)3
12:334:22584:15:22:301:430:548:15:Times:13:Courier:C:(N—Ni—i)(i+à1)'
12:335:22599:22:12:138:176:526:13:Courier:13:Courier:C:where
12:336:22604:12:24:268:291:514:13:Times:13:Times:A:a,, =

12:339:22645:20:23:282:291:447:16:Times:16:Times:C:=
12:340:22646:23:21:282:331:424:17:Helvetica:17:Helvetica:C:= L'VL
12:341:22652:21:20:137:562:403:11:Helvetica:14:Times:C:andFrepresentsthe mean returns, Visthe variance-covariance matrix and tisa
12:342:22726:20:18:137:562:383:14:Times:14:Times:C:vector of ones. The test statistic follows an F-distribution with (T—N1) and
12:343:22802:18:20:137:562:365:11:Times:13:Times:A:(N —N1—1)degrees of freedom where T is the number of observations, N is the
12:344:22877:20:18:137:562:345:13:Times:14:Times:A:total number of assets and N1 are the assets in r1.Theintuition behind the test
12:345:22956:18:20:136:562:327:14:Times:13:Times:A:is that a line originating from F,,inmean-variance space should have the same
12:346:23033:20:18:136:561:307:13:Times:14:Times:A:slope when it is tangent to the frontier of r1assetsand when it is tangent to the
12:347:23114:18:21:136:561:289:13:Times:13:Times:A:frontier of all rassets.The test statistic essentially measures the difference in the
12:348:23199:21:22:135:551:268:13:Times:13:Times:A:slopes. A more detailed description can be found in Jobson and Korkie (1989).
12:349:23276:22:19:159:562:246:14:Times:14:Times:C:Table 3 reports the results of the intersection tests. I report three differ-
12:350:23353:19:20:135:562:227:14:Times:14:Times:C:ent versions of the test statistic. The first is the statistic detailed in (3) with a
12:351:23438:20:18:135:562:207:14:Times:14:Times:C:heteroskedasticity-consistent variance-covariance matrix. The second and third
12:352:23516:18:20:135:561:189:13:Times:13:Times:A:versions correct for a moving-average process of 15 months using Bartlett weights
12:353:23597:20:18:135:560:169:13:Times:13:Times:A:[Newey and West (1987)J and Parzen weights [Andrews (1991)J. Given the evi-
12:354:23672:18:19:134:563:151:13:Times:13:Times:A:dence of nonnormality of the emerging market returns, it is important to assess the
12:355:23755:19:24:134:560:132:13:Times:13:Times:A:sensitivity of the intersection test to different assumptions on the error structure.5
12:356:23841:24:29:158:560:108:13:Times:13:Times:A:An alternative version of the test involves estimating (2) with the generalized
12:357:23920:29:0:341:352:79:11:Times:11:Times:F:10
13:358:23922:0:19:129:531:699:13:Times:13:Times:A:The results in table 3 unambiguously reject the null hypotheses that the two
13:359:23998:19:20:105:530:680:13:Times:13:Times:B:frontiers intersect. The first version of the test statistic provides evidence against
13:360:24084:20:18:105:531:660:14:Times:14:Times:C:the null with a p-value <0.1%. The correction for a possible moving-average
13:361:24159:18:19:105:532:642:13:Times:13:Times:B:structure has little effect on the inference. The p-values for the statistics are still
13:362:24246:19:19:106:533:623:13:Times:13:Times:A:<0.1% which supports the evidence against the null hypothesis. The intersection
13:363:24325:19:19:105:533:604:14:Times:14:Times:C:tests tells us that the shift in the frontier in figure 1 is genuine rather than an
13:364:24408:19:22:105:267:585:13:Times:13:Times:B:artifact of sampling variation.6
13:365:24440:22:19:129:532:563:13:Times:13:Times:A:The previous analysis assumes the portfolio manager had knowledge of the
13:366:24512:19:19:105:533:544:13:Times:13:Times:B:complete history of the returns in computing mean-variance efficient portfolios.
13:367:24592:19:19:105:533:525:14:Times:14:Times:C:Consider an alternative exercise. The investment in month t is determined by
13:368:24668:19:19:105:531:506:13:Times:13:Times:B:the weights of the unconditional minimum-variance portfolio estimated using five
13:369:24748:19:19:105:532:487:13:Times:13:Times:B:years of data ending in month t —1. The portfolio weights are updated each month
13:370:24828:19:19:105:531:468:13:Times:13:Times:B:and no short selling is allowed. Any survivorship bias in the pre-1981 data due to
13:371:24910:19:19:105:533:449:13:Times:13:Times:B:the backtracking of the indices is eliminated because the first investment occurs
13:372:24991:19:22:105:184:430:13:Times:13:Times:B:in March 1981.
13:373:25005:22:18:129:534:408:14:Times:14:Times:C:If the investor is limited to the 18 MSCI developed markets, this portfolio
13:374:25080:18:20:105:534:390:14:Times:14:Times:C:strategy delivers a 16.6% average return with a 15.8% standard deviation. If
13:375:25156:20:18:105:534:370:14:Times:14:Times:C:the emerging markets are added to the sample, the strategy produces a slightly
13:376:25234:18:19:106:531:352:14:Times:14:Times:C:higher return, 17.4%, but the standard deviation is much less, 11.4%. This out-
13:377:25313:19:20:105:532:333:13:Times:13:Times:B:of-sample analysis suggests that the main benefit to emerging market investment
13:378:25392:20:20:106:533:313:14:Times:14:Times:C:is not the increasing of returns but the lowering of the portfolio volatility for
13:379:25473:20:40:106:266:293:13:Times:13:Times:A:minimum variance strategies.7
13:380:25502:40:15:106:534:253:13:Times:13:Times:A:method of moments. However, the dimension of the weighting matrix is over 300
13:381:25579:15:14:106:532:238:13:Times:13:Times:A:which makes this method infeasible unless simplifying assumptions are made on
13:382:25656:14:15:106:533:224:13:Times:13:Times:A:the covariance structure. See the discussions in Ferson, Foerster and Keim (1993)
13:383:25737:15:10:106:205:209:13:Times:13:Times:A:and Ferson (1993).
13:384:25755:10:17:119:534:199:10:Times:14:Times:C:6Thisconsistent with recent results of De Santis (1993) who investigates
13:385:25827:17:15:107:534:182:14:Times:14:Times:C:whether the stochastic discount factor that prices the developed country returns
13:386:25907:15:11:107:323:167:13:Times:13:Times:A:also prices the emerging country returns.
13:387:25948:11:17:131:535:156:13:Times:13:Times:A:The minimum-variance portfolio strategy does not use the mean return vector
13:388:26023:17:13:108:532:139:13:Times:13:Times:A:[see Roll (1977, A.12—A.14)J. Harvey (1993c) considers alternative strategies that
13:389:26105:13:14:107:532:126:13:Times:14:Times:A:target 16% conditional volatility with information affecting all the moments.The
13:390:26185:14:15:107:535:112:14:Times:14:Times:C:results indicate that reward to risk ratios are greatly increased when emerging
13:391:26264:15:27:107:412:97:13:Times:13:Times:A:market investments augment developed market portfolios.
13:392:26319:27:0:314:325:70:11:Times:11:Times:F:11
14:393:26321:0:32:137:282:709:12:Helvetica:13:Times:A:3.2 Theissue of integration
14:394:26348:32:19:136:562:677:13:Times:13:Times:A:What is the risk of investing in emerging markets? How are these risks rewarded
14:395:26427:19:18:137:563:658:13:Times:13:Times:A:in equilibrium? Is it appropriate to consider emerging markets as additional assets
14:396:26510:18:20:137:563:640:13:Times:13:Times:A:that can be inserted into some well-known international asset pricing paradigm?
14:397:26589:20:19:136:565:620:13:Times:13:Times:A:These questions cannot be answered without some discussion of international cap-
14:398:26669:19:22:137:259:601:13:Times:13:Times:A:ital market integration.
14:399:26693:22:20:161:562:579:14:Times:14:Times:C:There are numerous international asset pricing models that take complete
14:400:26765:20:19:137:564:559:14:Times:14:Times:C:market integration as the null hypothesis. Stulz (1981) proposes a model where
14:401:26843:19:21:136:565:540:13:Times:13:Times:A:expected returns are linear in a measure of world consumption risk. Solnik (1974)
14:402:26924:21:18:137:563:519:14:Times:14:Times:C:pursues a global Sharpe (1964), Lintner (1965), and Black (1972) capital asset
14:403:27002:18:19:136:563:501:14:Times:14:Times:C:pricing model (CAPM) where investors in various countries differ in their con-
14:404:27080:19:19:136:562:482:14:Times:14:Times:C:sumption baskets and care about returns measured in their domestic currency.
14:405:27156:19:19:136:562:463:13:Times:13:Times:A:Adler and Dumas (1983) build on this model by incorporating stochastic national
14:406:27235:19:19:136:563:444:14:Times:14:Times:C:inflation. The single risk factor is the return on a value-weighted world equity
14:407:27315:19:19:136:564:425:14:Times:14:Times:C:market portfolio, hedged against currency risk. A final approach is a multi-beta
14:408:27395:19:19:135:565:406:14:Times:14:Times:C:specification in motivated by Merton (1973), Ross (1976) and Solnik (1983) in
14:409:27472:19:22:136:552:387:13:Times:13:Times:A:which expected returns are determined by sensitivities to many sources of risk.
14:410:27551:22:19:160:562:365:14:Times:14:Times:C:All of these models, assume that capital markets are integrated. Rejections
14:411:27626:19:19:135:561:346:13:Times:13:Times:A:of model restrictions could be interpreted in many ways. The rejection could be
14:412:27705:19:20:135:563:327:14:Times:14:Times:C:a result of some fundamental problem in testing the model such as improperly
14:413:27781:20:18:135:561:307:13:Times:13:Times:A:specifying the factor mimicking portfolios. Inference is especially complicated by
14:414:27863:18:18:135:562:289:13:Times:13:Times:A:Roll's (1977) critique. Rejection could also be due to the incomplete integration
14:415:27944:18:23:135:417:271:13:Times:13:Times:A:of the national market into the global capital market.
14:416:27998:23:20:159:563:248:14:Times:14:Times:C:There are reasons to doubt that some of the emerging markets are fully in-
14:417:28072:20:19:135:561:228:13:Times:13:Times:A:tegrated into world capital markets. Complete integration means that two assets
14:418:28151:19:18:134:561:209:14:Times:14:Times:C:with the same risk in different markets have the same expected returns. Factors
14:419:28230:18:19:134:561:191:14:Times:14:Times:C:that contribute to market integration are free access by foreigners to domestic
14:420:28309:19:19:134:561:172:13:Times:13:Times:A:capital markets and free access by domestic investors to foreign capital markets.
14:421:28390:19:18:134:559:153:13:Times:13:Times:A:Potential barriers to integration come in the form of: access, taxes and informa-
14:422:28471:18:25:134:157:135:13:Times:13:Times:A:tion.
14:423:28476:25:32:158:561:110:13:Times:13:Times:A:The appendix provides a country-by-country examination of the restrictions
14:424:28550:32:0:341:352:78:11:Times:11:Times:F:12
15:425:28552:0:21:96:520:696:14:Times:14:Times:C:that foreign investors face as of the Spring of 1993. The degrees of restrictions
15:426:28633:21:17:96:521:675:13:Times:13:Times:A:vary from completely closed to foreign investors (Nigeria) to100% investable (nine
15:427:28715:17:20:96:520:658:13:Times:13:Times:A:countries). The appendix also provides some details on a new set of indices de-
15:428:28794:20:18:96:522:638:13:Times:13:Times:A:veloped by the IFC called "investable" indices. While only a short history exists
15:429:28875:18:22:97:522:620:13:Times:13:Times:B:(since 1988), these new indices explicitly account for access restrictions on foreign
15:430:28960:22:18:96:167:598:13:Times:13:Times:A:participation.
15:431:28974:18:20:120:524:580:13:Times:13:Times:A:There are many other factors that either enhance or decrease the chance that
15:432:29050:20:20:97:521:560:13:Times:13:Times:B:the market is integrated. These issues include taxes, the timeliness of trading infor-
15:433:29136:20:19:97:524:540:13:Times:13:Times:B:mation, foreign exchange regulations, the availability and accuracy of accounting
15:434:29217:19:19:97:521:521:13:Times:13:Times:B:information, the number of securities cross-listed on developed exchanges, politi-
15:435:29299:19:19:97:524:502:14:Times:14:Times:C:cal risk, and the institutional structures than protect investors. In general, there
15:436:29383:19:19:98:523:483:14:Times:14:Times:C:is a trend towards relaxing the factors that contribute to segmentation in many
15:437:29462:19:19:98:523:464:13:Times:13:Times:B:emerging markets.8 However, the degree of integration likely varies across different
15:438:29546:19:18:98:523:445:13:Times:13:Times:B:countries. As such, it is unlikely that any asset pricing model which assumes com-
15:439:29628:18:19:98:523:427:13:Times:13:Times:B:plete integration of capital markets will be able to fully account for thebehavior
15:440:29710:19:54:98:328:408:13:Times:13:Times:B:of security prices in these different markets.
15:441:29756:54:29:99:190:354:13:Times:13:Times:B:3.3 Risk erposure
15:442:29773:29:20:123:524:325:13:Times:13:Times:A:In completely segmented markets where a one-factor model characterizes as-
15:443:29847:20:18:99:524:305:14:Times:14:Times:C:set returns, expected returns are priced with respect to the covariancewith the
15:444:29926:18:20:100:527:287:13:Times:13:Times:B:national market portfolio rather than the world market portfolio. In this case, the
15:445:30009:20:21:100:524:267:13:Times:13:Times:B:expected returns on the national index would be related to the volatilityof the na-
15:446:30092:21:19:100:527:246:13:Times:13:Times:B:tional market portfolio. In completely integrated capital markets with purchasing
15:447:30173:19:18:100:525:227:13:Times:13:Times:B:power parity, the covariance with theworld portfolio determines the cross-section
15:448:30254:18:20:100:524:209:13:Times:13:Times:B:of expected returns. It is reasonable to expect that some of the emergingmarkets
15:449:30334:20:22:100:524:189:13:Times:13:Times:B:are segmented, however, the degree and effect of the segmentationis not obvious.
15:450:30414:22:19:125:524:167:14:Times:13:Times:A:Even given this suspicion of some market segmentation, it is stillinforma-
15:451:30488:19:20:101:525:148:14:Times:15:Times:C:tive to assess the exposure of these markets to known global riskfactors. In a
15:452:30566:20:35:101:526:128:13:Times:13:Times:B:completely segmented capital market, covariance with a globalrisk factor may be
15:453:30645:35:26:114:336:93:13:Times:13:Times:A:8 See Bekaert (1993) and Harvey (1993a).
15:454:30685:26:0:308:320:67:12:Times:12:Times:F:13
16:455:30687:0:18:137:564:710:13:Times:13:Times:A:important. It is unlikely that the national economy is completely independent of
16:456:30767:18:23:137:238:692:13:Times:13:Times:A:the world economy.
16:457:30785:23:19:161:562:669:13:Times:13:Times:A:Table 4 provides two sets of linear factor model estimates. In panels A and
16:458:30860:19:20:137:563:650:14:Times:14:Times:C:B, both full sample and the most recent subperiod estimates of a single factor
16:459:30938:20:18:137:561:630:13:Times:13:Times:A:Sharpe-Lintner specification are presented. The single factor is the excess return
16:460:31020:18:19:136:564:612:13:Times:13:Times:A:on the MSCI world market portfolio. All returns are calculated in U.S. dollars in
16:461:31101:19:19:136:562:593:13:Times:13:Times:A:excess of an Eurodollar deposit with 30 days to maturity. This model is consistent
16:462:31183:19:18:136:563:574:13:Times:13:Times:A:with the world CAPM investigated in Cumby and Glen (1990), Harvey (1991) and
16:463:31259:18:25:136:385:556:13:Times:13:Times:A:one of the models in Dumas and Solnik (1993).
16:464:31304:25:18:160:561:531:13:Times:13:Times:A:In the other panels, a two-factor specification, motivated by Adler and Du-
16:465:31379:18:20:136:560:513:13:Times:13:Times:A:mas (1983), is presented adding the excess return on a trade-weighted portfolio
16:466:31458:20:20:135:562:493:13:Times:13:Times:A:of 10 currency deposits. Adler and Dumas' model allows for deviations from pur-
16:467:31537:20:18:135:562:473:14:Times:14:Times:C:chasing power parity. In their equation 14 with L countries, expected returns in
16:468:31617:18:18:135:563:455:14:Times:14:Times:C:a numeraire currency are generated by the covariance with the world portfolio
16:469:31694:18:20:135:561:437:13:Times:13:Times:A:and by the covariances of the asset returns and inflation rates in all the countries.
16:470:31779:20:19:135:562:417:14:Times:14:Times:C:The weights on these inflation covariances depend on the wealth-weighted risk
16:471:31856:19:18:135:563:398:13:Times:13:Times:A:aversion in each country. The usual way to implement this model is to follow the
16:472:31936:18:19:134:560:380:13:Times:13:Times:A:Solnik (1974) assumption that the asset covariance with the numeraire country's
16:473:32015:19:18:134:562:361:13:Times:13:Times:A:inflation is zero: Expected returns can then be written in terms of their covariance
16:474:32099:18:24:134:560:343:13:Times:13:Times:A:with the world portfolio and their L —1covariances with exchange rate changes.9
16:475:32178:24:19:158:561:319:13:Times:13:Times:A:Econometrically, this model is intractable unless a very small number of coun-
16:476:32256:19:19:134:561:300:14:Times:14:Times:C:tries are examined. For example, Dumas and Solnik (1993) are able to estimate
16:477:32333:19:19:134:562:281:13:Times:13:Times:A:the model for only four countries. One possible simplification pursued in a number
16:478:32415:19:19:133:560:262:14:Times:14:Times:C:of papers'° is to aggregate the exchange rate factor. Given that it is impossible
16:479:32496:19:19:133:559:243:13:Times:13:Times:A:to observe the wealth-weighted risk aversions of the L —1countries, trade weights
16:480:32577:19:23:134:442:224:13:Times:13:Times:A:(exports plus imports) are used as an aggregation method.
16:481:32634:23:19:157:558:201:14:Times:14:Times:C:The aggregation of the exchange risk factor departs from the asset pricing
16:482:32708:19:29:133:559:182:13:Times:13:Times:A:theory but provides tractability. One may also view this as the prespecification of
16:483:32791:29:8:156:346:153:13:Times:13:Times:A:See the discussion in Dumas (1993).
16:484:32826:8:9:141:174:145:9:Times:10:Helvetica:F:10See
16:485:32831:9:13:178:558:136:13:Times:13:Times:A:Bodurtha (1990), Jorion (1991), Bailey and Jagtiani (1993), Brown and
16:486:32900:13:14:133:560:123:14:Times:14:Times:C:Otsuki (1993), Ferson and Harvey (1993, 1994a) and Harvey, Solnik and Zhou
16:487:32974:14:29:133:169:109:12:Times:12:Times:F:(1993).
16:488:32981:29:0:339:350:80:11:Times:11:Times:F:14
17:489:32983:0:19:102:528:694:13:Times:13:Times:B:factors in some general multibeta model. Empirically, Ferson and Harvey (1993,
17:490:33061:19:19:102:529:675:13:Times:13:Times:B:1994a) and Harvey, Solnik and Zhou (1993) have found the aggregated exchange
17:491:33137:19:18:102:528:656:14:Times:14:Times:C:risk factor to be significant in both conditional and unconditional asset pricing
17:492:33218:18:20:102:529:638:14:Times:14:Times:C:tests. Indeed, Harvey, Solnik and Zhou show that the loadings from these first
17:493:33296:20:21:102:528:618:14:Times:14:Times:C:two factors are able to explain 50% of the cross-section of expected returns in
17:494:33375:21:21:102:202:597:13:Times:13:Times:B:developed markets.
17:495:33393:21:19:126:527:576:13:Times:13:Times:A:In contrast to previous applications which use exchange rate changes, I cal-
17:496:33469:19:18:103:530:557:14:Times:14:Times:C:culate the excess return on the trade-weighted currency portfolio. The portfolio
17:497:33549:18:20:103:531:539:14:Times:14:Times:C:return is a trade-weighted sum of investments in 10 currencies (G-10 countries
17:498:33627:20:19:103:529:519:13:Times:13:Times:B:minus the U.S. and plus Switzerland). The investment includes both the change
17:499:33704:19:19:104:530:500:13:Times:13:Times:B:in the value of the currency and the country's 30-day Eurodeposit rate. While the
17:500:33785:19:19:104:530:481:14:Times:14:Times:C:measure does not include emerging markets, the trade-weight on these markets
17:501:33861:19:19:104:528:462:14:Times:14:Times:C:would be very small." The foreign currency portfolio return is measured in ex-
17:502:33939:19:18:104:529:443:13:Times:14:Times:B:cess of the 30-day Eurodollar deposit rate. This procedure ensures thatthe factor
17:503:34020:18:19:104:530:425:14:Times:14:Times:C:return is a traded asset and avoids the two-step estimation problem for factor
17:504:34098:19:22:104:490:406:13:Times:13:Times:B:mimicking portfolios inherent in the Ferson and Harvey (1993) approach.
17:505:34169:22:18:128:530:384:14:Times:14:Times:C:In contrast to the results of Cumby and Glen (1990) and Harvey (1991)
17:506:34238:18:20:105:531:366:14:Times:14:Times:C:for developed countries, the world market portfolio beta has little influence on
17:507:34318:20:19:105:532:346:14:Times:14:Times:C:the expected returns in emerging countries. Only one country, Portugal, has a
17:508:34395:19:21:105:530:327:13:Times:13:Times:B:beta greater than one.12 The world market portfolio is significant in only seven
17:509:34475:21:19:105:532:306:13:Times:13:Times:B:countries: Mexico, Korea, Philippines, Malaysia, Thailand, Greece and Portugal.
17:510:34554:19:19:105:530:287:14:Times:13:Times:B:Of the emerging markets sample, many conjecture that these countries arethe
17:511:34629:19:22:105:307:268:13:Times:13:Times:B:most integrated in the world economy.
17:512:34666:22:28:130:530:246:14:Times:14:Times:C:Looking across all the countries, the world CAPM is rejected.The inter-
17:513:34737:28:14:130:533:218:13:Times:13:Times:A:Although some emerging markets have had very large exchange rate swings,
17:514:34809:14:15:106:530:204:13:Times:13:Times:A:the factor is intended to measure the return on a currency deposit ineach country.
17:515:34891:15:14:106:531:189:13:Times:13:Times:A:Presumably, a large depreciation in currency, say in Brazil, wouldbe offset by a
17:516:34971:14:15:106:531:175:14:Times:14:Times:C:large local deposit interest rate. Additional details ofthe construction and the
17:517:35051:15:10:106:329:160:13:Times:13:Times:A:data used are provided in Harvey (1993b).
17:518:35092:10:16:115:533:150:9:Times:13:Times:A:12also estimated [not reported] betas following Scholes and Williams (1977)
17:519:35167:16:15:107:533:134:13:Times:14:Times:A:using one lag and one lead of the world market return.In general, there is little
17:520:35248:15:13:107:531:119:13:Times:13:Times:A:difference between the Scholes-Williams betas (and intercepts) andthe OLS pa-
17:521:35325:13:16:107:532:106:13:Times:14:Times:A:rameters. However, three countries over the full sample have betas greaterthan
